<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Islamic Golden Age</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Period of cultural flourishing in the 8th to 14th centuries</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ededed;">Islamic Golden Age</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2" style="padding-bottom: 0.4em; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa; font-weight: bold;">8th century – 14th century</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Monarch(s)</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Leader(s)</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Umar_II" title="Umar II">Umar II</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil" title="Al-Mutawakkil">Al-Mutawakkil</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ismail_Samani" title="Ismail Samani">Ismail Samani</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Almanzor" title="Almanzor">Almanzor</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin Ayyubi</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baibars" title="Baibars">Baibars</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2">
<table style="width:100%;border-top: 1px solid #aaa;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 50%; padding: 0.2em 0.1em 0.2em 0; text-align: left;">← <b>Preceded by</b><br/><a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>
</td>
<td style="width: 50%; padding: 0.2em 0 0.2em 0.1em; text-align: right;"><b>Followed by</b> →<br/><a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid Renaissance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Islamic_Gunpowders" title="Age of the Islamic Gunpowders">Age of the Islamic Gunpowders</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Islamic Golden Age</b> was a period of cultural, economic, and scientific flourishing in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">history of Islam</a>, traditionally dated from the 8th century to the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saliba_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-3">[3]</a></sup> This period is traditionally understood to have begun during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> caliph <a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a> (786 to 809) with the inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, the world's largest city by then, where Muslim <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">scholars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymaths</a> from various parts of the world with different cultural backgrounds were mandated to gather and translate all of the known world's classical knowledge into <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gutas_1998_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gutas_1998-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The period is traditionally said to have ended with the collapse of the Abbasid caliphate due to <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol invasions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">Siege of Baghdad</a> in 1258.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahir_Abbas_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahir_Abbas-5">[5]</a></sup> A few scholars date the end of the golden age around 1350 linking with the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid Renaissance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoSM_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-HoSM-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Companion_to_the_Worlds_of_the_Renaissance_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Companion_to_the_Worlds_of_the_Renaissance-7">[7]</a></sup> while several modern historians and scholars place the end of the Islamic Golden Age as late as the end of 15th to 16th centuries meeting with the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_empires" title="Gunpowder empires">gunpowder empires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saliba_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-King-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-3">[3]</a></sup> The medieval period of Islam drew over a similar if not identical period, with one source defining it as 900–1300 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barlow-AofIW_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-AofIW-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_concepts">History of the concepts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maqamat_hariri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="989" data-file-width="974" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maqamat_hariri.jpg/220px-Maqamat_hariri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maqamat_hariri.jpg/330px-Maqamat_hariri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maqamat_hariri.jpg/440px-Maqamat_hariri.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maqamat_hariri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scholars at an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> library, from the <a href="/wiki/Maqama" title="Maqama">Maqamat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Hariri_of_Basra" title="Al-Hariri of Basra">al-Hariri</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yahya_ibn_Mahmud_al-Wasiti" title="Yahya ibn Mahmud al-Wasiti">Yahya ibn Mahmud al-Wasiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, 1237.</div></div></div>
<p>The metaphor of a <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_(metaphor)" title="Golden age (metaphor)">golden age</a> began to be applied in 19th-century literature about <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">Islamic history</a>, in the context of the western aesthetic fashion known as <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalism</a>. The author of a <i>Handbook for Travelers in Syria and Palestine</i> in 1868 observed that the most beautiful mosques of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> were "like Mohammedanism itself, now rapidly decaying" and relics of "the golden age of Islam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>
There is no unambiguous definition of the term, and depending on whether it is used with a focus on cultural or on military achievement, it may be taken to refer to rather disparate time spans. Thus, one 19th century author would have it extend to the duration of the caliphate, or to "six and a half centuries",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> while another would have it end after only a few decades of Rashidun conquests, with the death of <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">First Fitna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:217px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg/215px-Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg/323px-Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg/430px-Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg 2x" width="215"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Astrolabe_planisf%C3%A9rique_closeup800x600x300.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Improvements to the <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a> was one of the achievements of this era</div></div></div>
<p>During the early 20th century, the term was used only occasionally and often referred to as the early military successes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphs" title="Rashidun Caliphs">Rashidun caliphs</a>. It was only in the second half of the 20th century that the term came to be used with any frequency, now mostly referring to the cultural flourishing of science and mathematics under the caliphates during the 9th to 11th centuries (between the establishment of organised scholarship in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">crusades</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> but often extended to include part of the late 8th or the 12th to early 13th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Definitions may still vary considerably. Equating the end of the golden age with the end of the caliphates is a convenient cut-off point based on a historical landmark, but it can be argued that Islamic culture had entered a gradual decline much earlier; thus, Khan (2003) identifies the proper golden age as being the two centuries between 750 and 950, arguing that the beginning loss of territories under Harun al-Rashid worsened after the death of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">al-Ma'mun</a> in 833, and that the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">crusades</a> in the 12th century resulted in a weakening of the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Islamic empire</a> from which it never recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/220px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/330px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/440px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphates</a>, 622–750. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a1584e; color:white;"> </span> Expansion under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, 622–632</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef9070; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, 632–661</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fad07d; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, 661–750</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_influence">Religious influence</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_attitudes_towards_science" title="Islamic attitudes towards science">Islamic attitudes towards science</a></div>
<p>The various <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> injunctions and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> (or actions of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>), which place values on education and emphasize the importance of acquiring knowledge, played a vital role in influencing the Muslims of this age in their search for knowledge and the development of the body of science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hans45_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hans45-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_sponsorship">Government sponsorship</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Empire" title="Islamic Empire">Islamic Empire</a> heavily patronized scholars. The money spent on the <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Arabic_translation_movement" title="Graeco-Arabic translation movement">Translation Movement</a> for some translations is estimated to be equivalent to about twice the annual research budget of the United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(United_Kingdom)" title="Medical Research Council (United Kingdom)">Medical Research Council</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-18">[18]</a></sup> The best scholars and notable translators, such as <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a>, had salaries that are estimated to be the equivalent of professional athletes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc2_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">library</a> established in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> by Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur" title="Al-Mansur">al-Mansur</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> in 825 modeled after the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Gondishapur" title="Academy of Gondishapur">academy of Jundishapur</a>. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Openness_to_diverse_influences">Openness to diverse influences</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greek_contributions_to_the_Islamic_world" title="Greek contributions to the Islamic world">Greek contributions to the Islamic world</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_influence_on_Islamic_science" title="Indian influence on Islamic science">Indian influence on Islamic science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_influences_on_the_Islamic_world" title="Christian influences on the Islamic world">Christian influences on the Islamic world</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_influences_on_Islamic_pottery" title="Chinese influences on Islamic pottery">Chinese influences on Islamic pottery</a></div>
<p>During this period, the Muslims showed a strong interest in assimilating the scientific knowledge of the civilizations that had been conquered. Many classic works of antiquity that might otherwise have been lost were translated from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> into Syriac and Arabic, some of which were later in turn translated into other languages like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gutas_1998_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gutas_1998-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg/170px-Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg/255px-Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg/340px-Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hunayn_ibn-Ishaq_al-%27Ibadi_Isagoge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Christian physician <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a> lead the translation of works</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, especially the adherents of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian" title="Nestorian">Nestorians</a>), contributed to Islamic civilization during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Ummayads</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasids" title="Abbasids">Abbasids</a> by translating works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophers" title="Greek philosophers">Greek philosophers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_science" title="Ancient science">ancient science</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Language" title="Syriac Language">Syriac</a> and afterwards to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_Language" title="Arabic Language">Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> They also excelled in many fields, in particular <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rashed_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rashed-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-23">[23]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yusuf_Al-Khuri" title="Yusuf Al-Khuri">Yusuf Al-Khuri</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Na%27ima_al-Himsi" title="Ibn Na'ima al-Himsi">Al Himsi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">Qusta ibn Luqa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Masawaiyh" title="Masawaiyh">Masawaiyh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoubertRocher1995_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-JoubertRocher1995-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_Eutychius_of_Alexandria" title="Patriarch Eutychius of Alexandria">Patriarch Eutychius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_article_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica_article-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jabril_ibn_Bukhtishu" title="Jabril ibn Bukhtishu">Jabril ibn Bukhtishu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>. For a long period of time the personal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physicians" title="Physicians">physicians</a> of the Abbasid Caliphs were often <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Among the most prominent Christian families to serve as physicians to the caliphs were the <a href="/wiki/Bukhtishu" title="Bukhtishu">Bukhtishu</a> dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Throughout the 4th to 7th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> scholarly work in the Greek and Syriac languages was either newly translated or had been preserved since the Hellenistic period. Among the prominent centers of learning and transmission of classical wisdom were Christian colleges such as the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Nisibis" title="School of Nisibis">School of Nisibis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Edessa" title="School of Edessa">School of Edessa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nestorian.org_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-nestorian.org-36">[36]</a></sup> the pagan center of learning in <a href="/wiki/Harran#Early_Islamic_Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and the renowned <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospital</a> and medical Academy of Gondishapur, which was the intellectual, theological and scientific center of the Church of the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Many scholars of the House of Wisdom were of Christian background and it was led by Christian physician <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a>, with the support of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">Byzantine medicine</a>. Many of the most important philosophical and scientific works of the ancient world were translated, including the work of <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a> also were a notably high proportion of <a href="/wiki/List_of_pre-modern_Iranian_scientists_and_scholars" title="List of pre-modern Iranian scientists and scholars">scientists</a> who contributed to the Islamic Golden Age. According to <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a>: "Culturally, politically, and most remarkable of all even religiously, the Persian contribution to this new Islamic civilization is of immense importance. The work of Iranians can be seen in every field of cultural endeavor, including Arabic poetry, to which poets of Iranian origin composing their poems in Arabic made a very significant contribution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>While cultural influence used to radiate outward from Baghdad, after the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol destruction</a> of the Abbasid Caliphate, Arab influence decreased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulliet_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-bulliet-43">[43]</a></sup> Iran and Central Asia, benefiting from increased cross-cultural access to East Asia under <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Mongol rule</a>, flourished and developed more distinctively from Arab influence, such as the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance" title="Timurid Renaissance">Timurid Renaissance</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subtelny_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-subtelny-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_technology">New technology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg/170px-ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg/255px-ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg/340px-ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ManuscriptAbbasid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A manuscript written on paper during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> Era.</div></div></div>
<p>With a new and easier <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_paper" title="History of paper">introduction of paper</a>, information was democratized to the extent that, for probably the first time in history, it became possible to make a living from only writing and selling books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The use of paper spread from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> into Muslim regions in the eighth century through mass production in <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloom8_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloom8-46">[46]</a></sup> arriving in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> (modern Spain and Portugal) in the 10th century. It was easier to manufacture than <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a>, less likely to crack than <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, and could absorb ink, making it difficult to erase and ideal for keeping records. Islamic paper makers devised assembly-line methods of hand-copying manuscripts to turn out editions far larger than any available in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> It was from these countries that the rest of the world learned to make paper from linen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">Madrasa</a></div>
<p>The centrality of scripture and its study in the Islamic tradition helped to make education a central pillar of the religion in virtually all times and places in the history of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> The importance of learning in the Islamic tradition is reflected in a number of hadiths attributed to Muhammad, including one that states "Seeking knowledge is obligatory upon every Muslim".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> This injunction was seen to apply particularly to scholars, but also to some extent to the wider Muslim public, as exemplified by the dictum of <a href="/wiki/Al-Zarnuji" title="Al-Zarnuji">al-Zarnuji</a>, "learning is prescribed for us all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> While it is impossible to calculate literacy rates in pre-modern Islamic societies, it is almost certain that they were relatively high, at least in comparison to their European counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Azhar_(inside)_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg/170px-Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg/255px-Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg/340px-Al-Azhar_%28inside%29_2006.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Azhar_(inside)_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Organized instruction in the Cairo <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">Al-Azhar Mosque</a> began in 978</div></div></div>
<p>Education would begin at a young age with study of <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, either at home or in a primary school, which was often attached to a mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> Some students would then proceed to training in <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a> (Quranic exegesis) and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a> (Islamic jurisprudence), which was seen as particularly important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-5"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> Education focused on memorization, but also trained the more advanced students to participate as readers and writers in the tradition of commentary on the studied texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-6"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> It also involved a process of <a href="/wiki/Socialization" title="Socialization">socialization</a> of aspiring scholars, who came from virtually all social backgrounds, into the ranks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">ulema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-7"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the first few centuries of Islam, educational settings were entirely informal, but beginning in the 11th and 12th centuries, the ruling elites began to establish institutions of higher religious learning known as <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a> in an effort to secure support and cooperation of the ulema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-8"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> Madrasas soon multiplied throughout the Islamic world, which helped to spread Islamic learning beyond urban centers and to unite diverse Islamic communities in a shared cultural project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-9"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> Nonetheless, instruction remained focused on individual relationships between students and their teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-10"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> The formal attestation of educational attainment, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ijaza" title="Ijaza">ijaza</a></i>, was granted by a particular scholar rather than the institution, and it placed its holder within a genealogy of scholars, which was the only recognized hierarchy in the educational system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-11"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> While formal studies in madrasas were open only to men, women of prominent urban families were commonly educated in private settings and many of them received and later issued <i>ijazas</i> in hadith studies, calligraphy and poetry recitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus-210_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus-210-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Working women learned religious texts and practical skills primarily from each other, though they also received some instruction together with men in mosques and private homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus-210_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus-210-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Madrasas were devoted principally to study of law, but they also offered other subjects such as theology, medicine, and mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus217_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus217-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The madrasa complex usually consisted of a mosque, boarding house, and a library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus217_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus217-52">[52]</a></sup> It was maintained by a <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a> (charitable endowment), which paid salaries of professors, stipends of students, and defrayed the costs of construction and maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus217_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus217-52">[52]</a></sup> The madrasa was unlike a modern college in that it lacked a standardized curriculum or institutionalized system of certification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus217_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus217-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims distinguished disciplines inherited from pre-Islamic civilizations, such as philosophy and medicine, which they called "sciences of the ancients" or "rational sciences", from Islamic religious sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-12"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> Sciences of the former type flourished for several centuries, and their transmission formed part of the educational framework in classical and medieval Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-13"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup> In some cases, they were supported by institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> in Baghdad, but more often they were transmitted informally from teacher to student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkey-ed_49-14"><a href="#cite_note-berkey-ed-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Al_Karaouine" title="University of Al Karaouine">University of Al Karaouine</a>, founded in 859 AD, is listed in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Guinness_Book_Of_Records" title="The Guinness Book Of Records">The Guinness Book Of Records</a></i> as the world's oldest degree-granting university.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a> was another early madrasa now recognized as a university. The madrasa is one of the relics of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">Fatimid</a> caliphate. The Fatimids traced their descent to Muhammad's daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">Fatimah</a> and named the institution using a variant of her honorific title <i>Al-Zahra</i> (the brilliant).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Organized instruction in the <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">Al-Azhar Mosque</a> began in 978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a></div>
<p>Juristic thought gradually developed in study circles, where independent scholars met to learn from a local master and discuss religious topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallaq_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-hallaq-58">[58]</a></sup> At first, these circles were fluid in their membership, but with time distinct regional <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">legal schools</a> crystallized around shared sets of methodological principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallaq_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-hallaq-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vikor_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-59">[59]</a></sup> As the boundaries of the schools became clearly delineated, the authority of their doctrinal tenets came to be vested in a master jurist from earlier times, who was henceforth identified as the school's founder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallaq_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-hallaq-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vikor_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-59">[59]</a></sup> In the course of the first three centuries of Islam, all legal schools came to accept the broad outlines of classical legal theory, according to which Islamic law had to be firmly rooted in the Quran and hadith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vikor_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The classical <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">theory of Islamic jurisprudence</a> elaborates how scriptures should be interpreted from the standpoint of linguistics and rhetoric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calder_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-calder-61">[61]</a></sup> It also comprises methods for establishing authenticity of hadith and for determining when the legal force of a scriptural passage is <a href="/wiki/Naskh_(tafsir)" title="Naskh (tafsir)">abrogated</a> by a passage revealed at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calder_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-calder-61">[61]</a></sup> In addition to the Quran and sunnah, the classical theory of Sunni fiqh recognizes two other sources of law: juristic consensus (<i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijmaʿ</a></i>) and analogical reasoning (<i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> It therefore studies the application and limits of analogy, as well as the value and limits of consensus, along with other methodological principles, some of which are accepted by only certain legal schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calder_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-calder-61">[61]</a></sup> This interpretive apparatus is brought together under the rubric of <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>, which refers to a jurist's exertion in an attempt to arrive at a ruling on a particular question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calder_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-calder-61">[61]</a></sup> The theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver_Shia" title="Twelver Shia">Twelver Shia</a> jurisprudence parallels that of Sunni schools with some differences, such as recognition of reason (<i><a href="/wiki/%27Aql" title="'Aql">ʿaql</a></i>) as a source of law in place of <i>qiyas</i> and extension of the notion of sunnah to include traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">imams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The body of substantive Islamic law was created by independent jurists (<a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">muftis</a>). Their legal opinions (<a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwas</a>) were taken into account by ruler-appointed <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">judges</a> who presided over <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q%C4%81%E1%B8%8D%C4%AB" title="Qāḍī">qāḍī</a>'s</i> courts, and by <i>maẓālim</i> courts, which were controlled by the ruler's council and administered criminal law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vikor_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calder_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-calder-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" title="Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a></div>
<p>Classical Islamic theology emerged from an early doctrinal controversy which pitted the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahl_al-hadith" title="Ahl al-hadith">ahl al-hadith</a></i> movement, led by <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>, who considered the Quran and authentic hadith to be the only acceptable authority in matters of faith, against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27tazilite" title="Mu'tazilite">Mu'tazilites</a> and other theological currents, who developed theological doctrines using rationalistic methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus-130-131_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus-130-131-64">[64]</a></sup> In 833 the caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">al-Ma'mun</a> tried to impose Mu'tazilite theology on all religious scholars and instituted an inquisition (<i><a href="/wiki/Mihna" title="Mihna">mihna</a></i>), but the attempts to impose a caliphal writ in matters of religious orthodoxy ultimately failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapidus-130-131_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus-130-131-64">[64]</a></sup> This controversy persisted until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ash%27ari" title="Al-Ash'ari">al-Ash'ari</a> (874–936) found a middle ground between Mu'tazilite rationalism and Hanbalite literalism, using the rationalistic methods championed by Mu'tazilites to defend most substantive tenets maintained by <i>ahl al-hadith</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blankinship_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-blankinship-65">[65]</a></sup> A rival compromise between rationalism and literalism emerged from the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Maturidi" title="Al-Maturidi">al-Maturidi</a> (d. c. 944), and, although a minority of scholars remained faithful to the early <i>ahl al-hadith</i> creed, <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'ari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> theology came to dominate Sunni Islam from the 10th century on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blankinship_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-blankinship-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sughrat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="561" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/200px-Sughrat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/300px-Sughrat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Sughrat.jpg/400px-Sughrat.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sughrat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Arabic manuscript from the 13th century depicting <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> (Soqrāt) in discussion with his pupils</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina" title="Ibn Sina">Ibn Sina</a> (Avicenna) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Rushd" title="Ibn Rushd">Ibn Rushd</a> (Averroes) played a major role in interpreting the works of Aristotle, whose ideas came to dominate the non-religious thought of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_world" title="Christian world">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim worlds</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a>, translation of philosophical texts from Arabic to Latin in Western Europe "led to the transformation of almost all philosophical disciplines in the medieval Latin world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-standford_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-standford-67">[67]</a></sup> The influence of Islamic philosophers in Europe was particularly strong in natural philosophy, psychology and metaphysics, though it also influenced the study of logic and ethics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-standford_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-standford-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span></h3>
<p>Ibn Sina argued his "<a href="/wiki/Floating_man" title="Floating man">Floating man</a>" thought experiment concerning <a href="/wiki/Self-awareness" title="Self-awareness">self-awareness</a>, in which a man prevented of sense experience by being blindfolded and free falling would still be aware of his existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Tufail" title="Ibn Tufail">Ibn Tufail</a> wrote the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Hayy_ibn_Yaqdhan" title="Hayy ibn Yaqdhan">Hayy ibn Yaqdhan</a></i> and in response <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> wrote the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Theologus_Autodidactus" title="Theologus Autodidactus">Theologus Autodidactus</a></i>. Both were concerning <a href="/wiki/Autodidacticism" title="Autodidacticism">autodidacticism</a> as illuminated through the life of a <a href="/wiki/Feral_child" title="Feral child">feral child</a> <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneously generated</a> in a cave on a <a href="/wiki/Desert_island" title="Desert island">desert island</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Mathematics in the medieval Islamic world">Mathematics in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra">Algebra</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Green_mosque_archway.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="817" data-file-width="733" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Green_mosque_archway.JPG/170px-Green_mosque_archway.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Green_mosque_archway.JPG/255px-Green_mosque_archway.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Green_mosque_archway.JPG/340px-Green_mosque_archway.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Green_mosque_archway.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns" title="Islamic geometric patterns">Geometric patterns</a>: an archway in the Sultan's lodge in the Ottoman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ye%C5%9Fil_Mosque" title="Yeşil Mosque">Green Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bursa" title="Bursa">Bursa</a>, Turkey (1424), its <a href="/wiki/Girih" title="Girih">girih</a> strapwork forming 10-point stars and pentagons</div></div></div>
<p>Persian mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%E1%B8%A5ammad_ibn_M%C5%ABs%C4%81_al-Khw%C4%81rizm%C4%AB" title="Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī">Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</a> played a significant role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu-Arabic_numerals" title="Hindu-Arabic numerals">Hindu-Arabic numerals</a>. He has been described as the father<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> or founder<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>.
</p><p>Another Persian mathematician, <a href="/wiki/Omar_Khayyam" title="Omar Khayyam">Omar Khayyam</a>, is credited with identifying the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">Analytic geometry</a>. Omar Khayyam found the general geometric solution of the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic equation</a>. His book <i>Treatise on Demonstrations of Problems of Algebra</i> (1070), which laid down the principles of algebra, is part of the body of Persian mathematics that was eventually transmitted to Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yet another Persian mathematician, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharaf_al-D%C4%ABn_al-T%C5%ABs%C4%AB" title="Sharaf al-Dīn al-Tūsī">Sharaf al-Dīn al-Tūsī</a>, found algebraic and numerical solutions to various cases of cubic equations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> He also developed the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry">Geometry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns" title="Islamic geometric patterns">Islamic geometric patterns</a></div>
<p>Islamic art makes use of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns" title="Islamic geometric patterns">geometric patterns</a> and symmetries in many of its art forms, notably in <a href="/wiki/Girih_tiles" title="Girih tiles">girih</a> tilings. These are formed using a set of five tile shapes, namely a regular <a href="/wiki/Decagon" title="Decagon">decagon</a>, an elongated <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagon</a>, a bow tie, a <a href="/wiki/Rhombus" title="Rhombus">rhombus</a>, and a regular <a href="/wiki/Pentagon" title="Pentagon">pentagon</a>. All the sides of these tiles have the same length; and all their angles are multiples of 36° (π/5 <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a>), offering fivefold and tenfold symmetries. The tiles are decorated with <a href="/wiki/Strapwork" title="Strapwork">strapwork</a> lines (girih), generally more visible than the tile boundaries. In 2007, the physicists <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lu" title="Peter Lu">Peter Lu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Steinhardt" title="Paul Steinhardt">Paul Steinhardt</a> argued that girih from the 15th century resembled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasicrystalline" title="Quasicrystalline">quasicrystalline</a> <a href="/wiki/Penrose_tiling" title="Penrose tiling">Penrose tilings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Elaborate geometric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zellige" title="Zellige">zellige</a> tilework is a distinctive element in <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_architecture" title="Moroccan architecture">Moroccan architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castera_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castera-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muqarnas" title="Muqarnas">Muqarnas</a> vaults are three-dimensional but were designed in two dimensions with drawings of geometrical cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jamsh%C4%ABd_al-K%C4%81sh%C4%AB" title="Jamshīd al-Kāshī">Jamshīd al-Kāshī</a>'s estimate of <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">pi</a> would not be surpassed for 180 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trigonometry">Trigonometry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acute_Triangle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Acute_Triangle.svg/170px-Acute_Triangle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Acute_Triangle.svg/255px-Acute_Triangle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Acute_Triangle.svg/340px-Acute_Triangle.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acute_Triangle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A triangle labelled with the components of the law of sines. Capital <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>B</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>C</i></span> are the angles, and lower-case <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> are the sides opposite them. (<span class="texhtml"><i>a</i></span> opposite <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i></span>, etc.)</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Mu%CA%BF%C4%81dh_al-Jayy%C4%81n%C4%AB" title="Ibn Muʿādh al-Jayyānī">Ibn Muʿādh al-Jayyānī</a> is one of the several Islamic mathematicians on whom the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_sines" title="Law of sines">law of sines</a> is attributed; he wrote "<i>The Book of Unknown Arcs of a Sphere</i>" in the 11th century. This formula relates the lengths of the sides of any triangle, rather than only <a href="/wiki/Right_triangle" title="Right triangle">right triangles</a>, to the sines of its angles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> According to the law,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\sin A}{a}}\,=\,{\frac {\sin B}{b}}\,=\,{\frac {\sin C}{c}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
