<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i>, <i>b</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> are the lengths of the sides of a triangle, and <span class="texhtml"><i>A</i>, <i>B</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>C</i></span> are the opposite angles (see figure).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculus">Calculus</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> discovered the sum formula for the fourth power, using a method that could be generally used to determine the sum for any integral power. He used this to find the volume of a <a href="/wiki/Paraboloid" title="Paraboloid">paraboloid</a>. He could find the integral formula for any polynomial without having developed a general formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_sciences">Natural sciences</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Science in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_method">Scientific method</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (Alhazen) was a significant figure in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_scientific_method" title="History of scientific method">history of scientific method</a>, particularly in his approach to experimentation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElBizri2005a_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElBizri2005a-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haq_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haq-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabra_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabra-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and has been described as the "world's first true scientist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avicenna made rules for testing the effectiveness of drugs, including that the effect produced by the experimental drug should be seen constantly or after many repetitions, to be counted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-aramco-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhazes" title="Rhazes">Rhazes</a> was an early proponent of experimental medicine and recommended using control for clinical research. He said: "If you want to study the effect of bloodletting on a condition, divide the patients into two groups, perform bloodletting only on one group, watch both, and compare the results."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>Astronomy in Islam was able to grow greatly because of several key factors. One factor was geographically. The Islamic world was in close proximity to the ancient lands of the Greeks, which held valuable ancient knowledge of the heavens in Greek manuscripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> During the new <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Dynasty</a> after the movement of the capital in 762 AD to Baghdad, translators were sponsored to translate Greek texts into Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> This translation period led to many major scientific works from <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius</a> being translated into Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> From these translations previously lost knowledge of the cosmos was now being used to advance current astrological thinkers. The second key factor of astronomies growth was the religious observances followed by Muslims which expected them to pray at exact times during the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> These observances in timekeeping led to many questions in previous Greek mathematical astronomy, especially their timekeeping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1503" data-file-width="1633" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg/170px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg/255px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg/340px-Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Islamic_Art_sci_0161.1_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">Astrolabe</a> with Quranic inscriptions from Iran, dated 1060 <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">AH</a> (1650-51 AD)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">Astrolabe</a> was a Greek invention which was an important piece of Arabic astronomy. An Astrolabe is a handheld two-dimensional model of the sky which can solve problems of spherical astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> It is made up of lines of <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">azimuth</a> with an index, horizon, hour circle, zenith, <a href="/wiki/Rete_algorithm" title="Rete algorithm">Rete</a>, star pointer, and equator to accurately show where the stars are at that given moment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-6"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> Use of the astrolabe is best expressed in <a href="/wiki/Al-Farghani" title="Al-Farghani">Al-Farghani</a>'s treatise on the astrolabe due to the mathematical way he applied the instrument to astrology, astronomy, and timekeeping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-7"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> The earliest known Astrolabe in existence today comes from the Islamic period. It was made by <a href="/wiki/Nastulus" title="Nastulus">Nastulus</a> in 927-28 AD and is now a treasure of the <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_National_Museum" title="Kuwait National Museum">Kuwait National Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-8"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In about 964 AD, the Persian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_al-Sufi" title="Abd al-Rahman al-Sufi">Abd al-Rahman al-Sufi</a>, writing in his <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Fixed_Stars" title="Book of Fixed Stars">Book of Fixed Stars</a></i>, described a "nebulous spot" in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andromeda_constellation" title="Andromeda constellation">Andromeda constellation</a>, the first definitive reference to what is now known to be the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_galaxies" title="List of nearest galaxies">nearest spiral galaxy</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.
</p><p>One of the largest advances in Islamic astronomy was the rejection of the Ptolemaic system of the planets. This system developed by Ptolemy placed the sun, moon, and other planets in orbit around the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-9"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> Ptolemy thought that the planets moved on circles called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicycles" title="Epicycles">epicycles</a> and that their centers rode on <a href="/wiki/Deferent_and_epicycle" title="Deferent and epicycle">deferents</a>. The deferents were <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, and the angular motion of a planet was uniform around the <a href="/wiki/Equant" title="Equant">equant</a> which was a point opposite the deferent center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-10"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> Simply, Ptolemy's models were a mathematical system for predicting the positions of the planets. One of the first to criticize this model was <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a>, a leader of physics in the 11th century in Cairo. Then in the 13th century <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a> constructed the <a href="/wiki/Maragheh_observatory" title="Maragheh observatory">Maragha Observatory</a> in what is today Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-11"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> Al-Tusi found the equant dissatisfying and replaced it by adding a geometrical technique called a <a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi-couple</a>, which generates linear motion from the sum of two circular motions. Then, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Shatir" title="Ibn al-Shatir">Ibn al-Shatir</a> who was working in Damascus in 1350 AD employed the Tusi-couple to successfully eliminate the equant as well as other objectionable circles that Ptolemy had used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This new model properly aligned the celestial spheres and was mathematically sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-12"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup> This development by Ibn al-Shatir, as well as the Maragha astronomers remained relatively unknown in medieval Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-13"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The Tusi couple was later employed in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Shatir" title="Ibn al-Shatir">Ibn al-Shatir</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric model</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a>' <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">heliocentric model</a> although it is not known who the intermediary is or if Copernicus rediscovered the technique independently. The names for some of the stars used, including <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rigel" title="Rigel">Rigel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aldebaran" title="Aldebaran">Aldebaran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fomalhaut" title="Fomalhaut">Fomalhaut</a> are several of the names that come directly from Arabic origins or are the translations of Ptolemy's Greek descriptions which are still in use today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingerich1986_92-14"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich1986-92">[92]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tusi-couple.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Tusi-couple.gif/220px-Tusi-couple.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Tusi-couple.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tusi-couple.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Physics in the medieval Islamic world">Physics in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> played a role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>. One of the prevailing theories of vision in his time and place was the <a href="/wiki/Emission_theory_(vision)" title="Emission theory (vision)">emission theory</a> supported by <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> and Ptolemy, where sight worked by the eye emitting rays of light, and the other was the Aristotelean theory that sight worked when the essence of objects flows into the eyes. Alhazen correctly argued that vision occurred when light, traveling in straight lines, reflects off an object into the eyes. <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> wrote of his insights into light, stating that its velocity must be immense when compared to the speed of sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Standrews_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Standrews-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Alchemy and chemistry in the medieval Islamic world">Alchemy and chemistry in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>The early Islamic period saw the establishment of some of the longest lived theoretical frameworks in <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfur-mercury_theory_of_metals" title="Sulfur-mercury theory of metals">sulfur-mercury theory of metals</a>, first attested in pseudo-Apollonius of Tyana's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirr_al-khaliqa" title="Sirr al-khaliqa"><i>Sirr al-khalīqa</i></a> ("The Secret of Creation", c. 750–850) and in the Arabic writings attributed to <a href="/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Hayyan" title="Jabir ibn Hayyan">Jābir ibn Ḥayyān</a> (written c. 850–950),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> would remain the basis of all theories of metallic composition until the eighteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Likewise, the <i><a href="/wiki/Emerald_Tablet" title="Emerald Tablet">Emerald Tablet</a></i>, a compact and cryptic text that all later alchemists up to and including <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1642–1727) would regard as the foundation of their art, first occurs in the <i>Sirr al-khalīqa</i> and in one of the works attributed to Jābir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Substantial advances were also made in practical chemistry. The works attributed to Jābir, and those of the Persian alchemist and physician <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Razi" title="Abu Bakr al-Razi">Abū Bakr al-Rāzī</a> (c. 865–925), contain the earliest known systematic classifications of chemical substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemicke-listy.cz_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-chemicke-listy.cz-98">[98]</a></sup> However, alchemists were not only interested in identifying and classifying chemical substances, but also in artificially creating them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Significant examples from the medieval Islamic world include the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a> from <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic substances</a> as described in the works attributed to Jābir,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and Abū Bakr al-Rāzī's experiments with <a href="/wiki/Vitriol" title="Vitriol">vitriol</a>, which would eventually lead to the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineral_acids" title="Mineral acids">mineral acids</a> like <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> by thirteenth century Latin alchemists such as <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Geber" title="Pseudo-Geber">pseudo-Geber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemicke-listy.cz_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-chemicke-listy.cz-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geodesy">Geodesy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Geography and cartography in the medieval Islamic world">Geography and cartography in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>Al-Biruni (973–1048) estimated the <a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius">radius of the earth</a> as 6339.6 km (modern value is c. 6,371 km), the best estimate at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medicine in the medieval Islamic world">Medicine in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/170px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/255px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/340px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The eye, according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunain_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunain ibn Ishaq">Hunain ibn Ishaq</a>. From a manuscript dated circa 1200.</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_system" title="Cardiovascular system">cardiovascular system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Commentary_on_Anatomy_in_Avicenna%27s_Canon" title="Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon">Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon</a></i> was the first known scholar to contradict the contention of the <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> School that blood could pass between the ventricles in the heart through the cardiac inter-ventricular septum that separates them, saying that there is no passage between the ventricles at this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-West-102">[102]</a></sup> Instead, he correctly argued that all the blood that reached the left ventricle did so after passing through the lung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-West-102">[102]</a></sup> He also stated that there must be small communications, or pores, between the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein">pulmonary vein</a>, a prediction that preceded the discovery of the pulmonary capillaries of <a href="/wiki/Marcello_Malpighi" title="Marcello Malpighi">Marcello Malpighi</a> by 400 years. The <i>Commentary</i> was rediscovered in the twentieth century in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prussian_State_Library" title="Prussian State Library">Prussian State Library</a> in Berlin; whether its view of the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> influenced scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Servetus" title="Michael Servetus">Michael Servetus</a> is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-West-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>, Rhazes stated that <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a> had <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sensory_neuron" title="Sensory neuron">sensory</a> functions, describing 7 <a href="/wiki/Cranial_nerves" title="Cranial nerves">cranial</a> and 31 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_nerves" title="Spinal nerves">spinal cord nerves</a>. He assigned a numerical order to the cranial nerves from the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Hypoglossal_nerve" title="Hypoglossal nerve">hypoglossal nerves</a>. He classified the spinal nerves into 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cervical_nerves" title="Cervical nerves">cervical</a>, 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thoracic_nerves" title="Thoracic nerves">thoracic</a>, 5 <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_nerves" title="Lumbar nerves">lumbar</a>, 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacral_nerves" title="Sacral nerves">sacral</a>, and 3 coccygeal nerves. He used this to link clinical signs of injury to the corresponding location of lesions in the nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nervous_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-nervous-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern commentators have likened medieval accounts of the "struggle for existence" in the animal kingdom to the framework of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_evolution" title="Theory of evolution">theory of evolution</a>.
Thus, in his survey of the history of the ideas which led to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conway_Zirkle" title="Conway Zirkle">Conway Zirkle</a> noted that <a href="/wiki/Al-Jahiz" title="Al-Jahiz">al-Jahiz</a> was one of those who discussed a "struggle for existence", in his <i><a href="/wiki/Kit%C4%81b_al-Hayaw%C4%81n" title="Kitāb al-Hayawān">Kitāb al-Hayawān</a></i> (Book of Animals), written in the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
In the 13th century, <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Nasir al-Din al-Tusi</a> believed that humans were derived from advanced animals, saying, "Such humans [probably anthropoid apes]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alakbarov_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alakbarov-105">[105]</a></sup> live in the Western Sudan and other distant corners of the world. They are close to animals by their habits, deeds and behavior."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alakbarov_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alakbarov-105">[105]</a></sup> In 1377, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Muqaddimah" title="Muqaddimah">Muqaddimah</a> stated, "The animal kingdom was developed, its species multiplied, and in the gradual process of Creation, it ended in man and arising from the world of the monkeys."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering">Engineering</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_inventions_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="List of inventions in the medieval Islamic world">List of inventions in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ban%C5%AB_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Banū Mūsā">Banū Mūsā</a> brothers, in their <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ingenious_Devices" title="Book of Ingenious Devices">Book of Ingenious Devices</a>, describe an <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">automatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a> player which may have been the first <a href="/wiki/Program_(machine)" title="Program (machine)">programmable machine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koetsier2_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koetsier2-107">[107]</a></sup> The flute sounds were produced through hot <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_power" title="Steam power">steam</a> and the user could adjust the device to various patterns so that they could get various sounds from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_sciences">Social sciences</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> is regarded to be among the founding fathers of modern <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndSociology_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndSociology-109">[n 1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndSociology_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndSociology-109">[n 1]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economics_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economics-111">[n 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archiving was a respected position during this time in Islam though most of the governing documents have been lost over time. However, from correspondence and remaining documentation gives a hint of the social climate as well as shows that the archives were detailed and vast during their time. All letters that were received or sent on behalf of the governing bodies were copied, archived and noted for filing. The position of the archivist was seen as one that had to have a high level of devotion as they held the records of all pertinent transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare">Healthcare</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hospitals">Hospitals</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">Bimaristan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_(1878)_-_TIMEA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg/220px-Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg/330px-Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg/440px-Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_%281878%29_-_TIMEA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Door_of_the_Moristan_of_Kala%27oon_(1878)_-_TIMEA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Qalawun_complex" title="Qalawun complex">Qalawun complex</a> which housed the notable Mansuri hospital in Cairo</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known Islamic hospital was built in 805 in Baghdad by order of Harun Al-Rashid, and the most important of Baghdad's hospitals was established in 982 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buyid" title="Buyid">Buyid</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/%27Adud_al-Dawla" title="'Adud al-Dawla">'Adud al-Dawla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI2-tibb_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-tibb-113">[111]</a></sup> The best documented early Islamic hospitals are the great Syro-Egyptian establishments of the 12th and 13th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI2-tibb_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-tibb-113">[111]</a></sup> By the tenth century, Baghdad had five more hospitals, while <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> had six hospitals by the 15th century and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">Córdoba</a> alone had 50 major hospitals, many exclusively for the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The typical hospital was divided into departments such as systemic diseases, surgery, and orthopedics, with larger hospitals having more diverse specialties. "Systemic diseases" was the rough equivalent of today's <a href="/wiki/Internal_medicine" title="Internal medicine">internal medicine</a> and was further divided into sections such as fever, infections and digestive issues. Every department had an officer-in-charge, a presiding officer and a supervising specialist. The hospitals also had lecture theaters and libraries. Hospitals staff included sanitary inspectors, who regulated cleanliness, and accountants and other administrative staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup> The hospitals were typically run by a three-man board comprising a non-medical administrator, the chief pharmacist, called the shaykh saydalani, who was equal in rank to the chief physician, who served as mutwalli (dean).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-aramco-90">[90]</a></sup> Medical facilities traditionally closed each night, but by the 10th century laws were passed to keep hospitals open 24 hours a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdouglas_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdouglas-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>For less serious cases, physicians staffed outpatient clinics. Cities also had first aid centers staffed by physicians for emergencies that were often located in busy public places, such as big gatherings for Friday prayers. The region also had mobile units staffed by doctors and pharmacists who were supposed to meet the need of remote communities. Baghdad was also known to have a separate hospital for convicts since the early 10th century after the vizier ‘Ali ibn Isa ibn Jarah ibn Thabit wrote to Baghdad's chief medical officer that "prisons must have their own doctors who should examine them every day". The first hospital built in Egypt, in Cairo's Southwestern quarter, was the first documented facility to care for mental illnesses. In <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>'s Arghun Hospital, care for mental illness included abundant light, fresh air, running water and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Medical students would accompany physicians and participate in patient care. Hospitals in this era were the first to require medical diplomas to license doctors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alatas_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alatas-116">[114]</a></sup> The licensing test was administered by the region's government appointed chief medical officer. The test had two steps; the first was to write a treatise, on the subject the candidate wished to obtain a certificate, of original research or commentary of existing texts, which they were encouraged to scrutinize for errors. The second step was to answer questions in an interview with the chief medical officer. Physicians worked fixed hours and medical staff salaries were fixed by law. For regulating the quality of care and arbitrating cases, it is related that if a patient dies, their family presents the doctor's prescriptions to the chief physician who would judge if the death was natural or if it was by negligence, in which case the family would be entitled to compensation from the doctor. The hospitals had male and female quarters while some hospitals only saw men and other hospitals, staffed by women physicians, only saw women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup> While women physicians practiced medicine, many largely focused on <a href="/wiki/Obstetrics" title="Obstetrics">obstetrics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0816_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0816-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>
Hospitals were forbidden by law to turn away patients who were unable to pay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdouglas_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-sdouglas-115">[113]</a></sup> Eventually, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charitable_foundation" title="Charitable foundation">charitable foundations</a> called <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqfs</a> were formed to support hospitals, as well as schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdouglas_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-sdouglas-115">[113]</a></sup> Part of the state budget also went towards maintaining hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-4"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup> While the services of the hospital were free for all citizens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdouglas_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-sdouglas-115">[113]</a></sup> and patients were sometimes given a small stipend to support recovery upon discharge, individual physicians occasionally charged fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-5"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup> In a notable endowment, a 13th-century governor of Egypt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Mansur_Qalawun" title="Al-Mansur Qalawun">Al-Mansur Qalawun</a> ordained a foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Qalawun_complex" title="Qalawun complex">Qalawun hospital</a> that would contain a mosque and a chapel, separate wards for different diseases, a library for doctors and a pharmacy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adler_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-adler-118">[116]</a></sup> and the hospital is used today for <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0317_114-6"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0317-114">[112]</a></sup> The Qalawun hospital was based in a former Fatimid palace which had accommodation for 8,000 people – <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> "it served 4,000 patients daily."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The waqf stated, </p><blockquote><p>... The hospital shall keep all patients, men and women, until they are completely recovered. All costs are to be borne by the hospital whether the people come from afar or near, whether they are residents or foreigners, strong or weak, low or high, rich or poor, employed or unemployed, blind or sighted, physically or mentally ill, learned or illiterate. There are no conditions of consideration and payment, none is objected to or even indirectly hinted at for non-payment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adler_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-adler-118">[116]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacies">Pharmacies</span></h3>
<p>Arabic scholars used their natural and cultural resources to contribute to the strong development of pharmacology. They believed that God had provided the means for a cure for every disease. However, there was confusion about the nature of some ancient plants that existed during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2015_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-King2015-120">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>A prominent figure that was influential in the development of pharmacy used the name Yūhannā ibn <a href="/wiki/Masawaiyh" title="Masawaiyh">Māsawaiyh</a> (circa 777-857). He was referred to as "The Divine Mesue" and "The Prince of Medicine" by European scholars. Māsawaiyh led the first private medical school in Baghdad and wrote three major pharmaceutical treatises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2013_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2013-121">[119]</a></sup> These treatises consisted of works over compound medicines, humors, and pharmaceutical recipes that provided instructions on how they were to be prepared. In the Latin West, these works were typically published together under the title "Opera Medicinalia" and were broken up into "De simplicubus", "Grabadin", and "Canones universales". Although <a href="/wiki/Masawaiyh" title="Masawaiyh">Māsawaiyh</a>'s influence was so significant that his writings became the most dominant source of pharmaceutical writings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2013_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2013-121">[119]</a></sup> his exact identity remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2013_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2013-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the past, all substances that were to be introduced into, on or near the human body were labeled as medicine, ranging from drugs, food, beverages, even perfumes to cosmetics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The earliest distinction between medicine and pharmacy as disciplines began in the seventh century, when pharmacists and apothecaries appeared in the first hospitals. Demand for drugs increased as the population increased. By the ninth century where pharmacy was established as an independent and well-defined profession by Muslim scholars. It is said by many historians that the opening of the first private pharmacy in the eighth century marks the independence of pharmacy from medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King2015_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-King2015-120">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of medicine and pharmacy within the Islamic caliphate by the ninth century occurred at the same time as rapid expansion of many scientific institutions, libraries, schools, hospitals and then pharmacies in many Muslim cities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The rise of <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> during the ninth century also played a vital role for early pharmacological development. While Arab pharmacists were not successful in converting non-precious metals into precious metals, their works giving details of techniques and lab equipment were major contributors to the development of pharmacy. Chemical techniques such as distillation, condensation, evaporation and pulverization were often used.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Qur'an provided the basis for the development of professional ethics where the rise of ritual washing also influenced the importance of hygiene in pharmacology. Pharmacies were periodically visited by government inspectors called <a href="/wiki/Muhtasib" title="Muhtasib">muhtasib</a>, who checked to see that the medicines were mixed properly, not diluted and kept in clean jars. Work done by the <a href="/wiki/Muhtasib" title="Muhtasib">muhtasib</a> was carefully outlined in manuals that explained ways of examining and recognizing falsified drugs, foods and spices. It was forbidden for pharmacists to perform medical treatment without the presence of a physician, while physicians were limited to the preparation and handling of medications. It was feared that recipes would fall into the hands of someone without the proper pharmaceutical training.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Licenses were required to run private practices. Violators were fined or beaten.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medicine in the medieval Islamic world">Medicine in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">Humorism</a> was largely dominant during this time. Arab physician <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Zuhr" title="Ibn Zuhr">Ibn Zuhr</a> provided proof that <a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">scabies</a> is caused by the <a href="/wiki/Sarcoptes_scabiei" title="Sarcoptes scabiei">itch mite</a> and that it can be cured by removing the parasite without the need for purging, bleeding or other treatments called for by humorism, making a break with the humorism of Galen and <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0816_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0816-117">[115]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhazes" title="Rhazes">Rhazes</a> differentiated through careful observation the two diseases <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, which were previously lumped together as a single disease that caused <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rashes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> This was based on location and the time of the appearance of the symptoms and he also scaled the degree of severity and prognosis of infections according to the color and location of rashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Zahrawi" title="Al-Zahrawi">Al-Zahrawi</a> was the first physician to describe an <a href="/wiki/Ectopic_pregnancy" title="Ectopic pregnancy">ectopic pregnancy</a>, and the first physician to identify the hereditary nature of <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia" title="Haemophilia">haemophilia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>On <a href="/wiki/Hygiene#Medical_hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygienic</a> practices, Rhazes, who was once asked to choose the site for a new hospital in Baghdad, suspended pieces of meat at various points around the city, and recommended building the hospital at the location where the meat putrefied the slowest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Razi is sometimes called the "Father of pediatrics" for writing the monograph, <i>The Diseases of Children</i> treating paediatrics as an independent field of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elgood2_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elgood2-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_scholars" title="Islamic scholars">Islamic scholars</a>, Indian and Greek physicians and medical researchers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, Mankah, <a href="/wiki/Atreya" title="Atreya">Atreya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charaka" title="Charaka">Charaka</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnivesa" title="Agnivesa">Agnivesa</a> were pre-eminent authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> In order to make the Indian and Greek tradition more accessible, understandable, and teachable, Islamic scholars ordered and made more systematic the vast Indian and Greco-Roman medical knowledge by writing encyclopedias and summaries. Sometimes, past scholars were criticized, like Rhazes who criticized and refuted Galen's revered theories, most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">Theory of Humors</a> and was thus accused of ignorance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncbi_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-ncbi-91">[91]</a></sup> It was through 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">Arabic translations</a> that medieval Europe rediscovered <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Hellenic medicine</a>, including the works of Galen and Hippocrates, and discovered <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">ancient Indian medicine</a>, including the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charaka" title="Charaka">Charaka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lock607_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lock607-128">[126]</a></sup> Works such as Avicenna's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> were translated into Latin and disseminated throughout Europe. During the 15th and 16th centuries alone, <i>The Canon of Medicine</i> was published more than thirty-five times. It was used as a standard medical textbook through the 18th century in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacb1_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacb1-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Zahrawi" title="Al-Zahrawi">Al-Zahrawi</a> was a tenth century <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Arab</a> physician. He is sometimes referred to as the "Father of surgery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anzjournal_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anzjournal-130">[128]</a></sup> He describes what is thought to be the first attempt at reduction <a href="/wiki/Mammaplasty" title="Mammaplasty">mammaplasty</a> for the management of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gynaecomastia" title="Gynaecomastia">gynaecomastia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anzjournal_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anzjournal-130">[128]</a></sup> and the first <a href="/wiki/Mastectomy" title="Mastectomy">mastectomy</a> to treat <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0816_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0816-117">[115]</a></sup> He is credited with the performance of the first <a href="/wiki/Thyroidectomy" title="Thyroidectomy">thyroidectomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> He wrote three textbooks on surgery, including <i>Manual of Medial Practitioners</i> which contains a catalog of 278 instruments used in surgery <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golden_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-golden-132">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the thirteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Quff" title="Ibn al-Quff">Ibn al-Quff</a> was a physician and surgeon who published numerous books, commentaries, treatises on surgery. Most notably, he wrote <i>Basics in the Art of Surgery</i>, a general medical manual covering anatomy, drugs therapy and surgical care, which was by far the largest Arabic text on surgery during the entire medieval period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIM_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIM-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commerce_and_travel">Commerce and travel</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islamic_economics" title="History of Islamic economics">History of Islamic economics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Geography and cartography in the medieval Islamic world">Geography and cartography in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1552" data-file-width="1424" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG/220px-Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG/330px-Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG/440px-Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Idrisi%27s_world_map.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Introductory summary overview map from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Idrisi" title="Al-Idrisi">al-Idrisi</a>'s 1154 world atlas (note that <a href="/wiki/South" title="South">South</a> is at the top of the map).</div></div></div>
<p>Apart from the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, navigable rivers were uncommon in the Middle East, so transport by sea was very important. Navigational sciences were highly developed, making use of a rudimentary <a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">sextant</a> (known as a kamal). When combined with detailed maps of the period, sailors were able to sail across oceans rather than skirt along the coast. Muslim sailors were also responsible for reintroducing large, three-masted merchant vessels to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The name <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a> may derive from an earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> boat known as the <i>qārib</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Muslims went to <a href="/wiki/Islam_during_the_Song_dynasty" title="Islam during the Song dynasty">China</a> to trade, and these Muslims began to have a great economic influence on the country. Muslims virtually dominated the import/export industry by the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sung_dynasty" title="Sung dynasty">Sung dynasty</a> (960–1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">Muhammad al-Idrisi</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Tabula_Rogeriana" title="Tabula Rogeriana">Tabula Rogeriana</a>, the best maps of the Middle Ages, used by various explorers such as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasco_Da_Gama" title="Vasco Da Gama">Vasco Da Gama</a> for their <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">voyages in America</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-houben_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-houben-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spread_sugarcane.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="483" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Spread_sugarcane.JPG/220px-Spread_sugarcane.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Spread_sugarcane.JPG/330px-Spread_sugarcane.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Spread_sugarcane.JPG/440px-Spread_sugarcane.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spread_sugarcane.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The diffusion of sugarcane from the Indian subcontinent to Spain during Islamic rule.</div></div></div>
<p>The Arabs of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> exerted a large impact on Spanish agriculture, including the restoration of Roman-era aqueducts and irrigation channels, as well as the introduction of new technologies such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Acequia" title="Acequia">acequias</a></i> (derived from the <a href="/wiki/Qanat" title="Qanat">qanats</a> of Persia) and <a href="/wiki/Persian_gardens" title="Persian gardens">Persian gardens</a> (such as at the <a href="/wiki/Generalife" title="Generalife">Generalife</a>). In Spain and Sicily, the Arabs introduced crops and foodstuffs from the Persia and India such as <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">oranges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">lemons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, saffron, carrots, apricots and eggplants, as well as restoring cultivation of olives and pomegranates from Greco-Roman times. The <a href="/wiki/Palmeral_of_Elche" title="Palmeral of Elche">Palmeral of Elche</a> in southern Spain is a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage site</a> that is emblematic of the Islamic agricultural legacy in Europe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_culture">Arts and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_poetry">Literature and poetry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_literature" title="Islamic literature">Islamic literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_poetry" title="Islamic poetry">Islamic poetry</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">Arabic literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></div>
<p>The 13th century <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuq</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> wrote some of the finest <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a> in the Persian language and remains one of the best selling poets in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Other famous poets of the Persian language include <a href="/wiki/Hafez" title="Hafez">Hafez</a> (whose work was read by William Jones, Thoreau, Goethe, Ralph Waldo Emerson and Friedrich Engels), <a href="/wiki/Saadi_Shirazi" title="Saadi Shirazi">Saadi</a> (whose poetry was cited extensively by Goethe, Hegel and Voltaire), <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omar_Khayyam" title="Omar Khayyam">Omar Khayyam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrow</a>.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i>, an anthology of Middle Eastern folk tales compiled in the Arabic language during the time of the Abbasid Caliphate, has had a large influence on Western and Middle Eastern literature and popular culture with such classics as <a href="/wiki/Aladdin" title="Aladdin">Aladdin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Baba_and_the_Forty_Thieves" title="Ali Baba and the Forty Thieves">Ali Baba and the Forty Thieves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sinbad_the_Sailor" title="Sinbad the Sailor">Sinbad the Sailor</a>. The folk-tale 'Sinbad the Sailor' even draws inspiration directly from Hellenistic literature like the <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric epics</a> (translated from Greek to Arabic in the 8th century CE) and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romances</a> (tales of Alexander the Great popular in Europe, the Middle East and India).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg/220px-WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg/330px-WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg/440px-WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WLA_vanda_Ottoman_marquetry_and_tile-top_table_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marquetry" title="Marquetry">Marquetry</a> and tile-top table, 1560</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">Calligraphy</a>, an essential aspect of written <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, developed in manuscripts and architectural decoration. This form of visual art can be found adorning the walls of palaces, the interior and domes of mosques as well as the surrounding structure of <a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAACMadden_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAACMadden-139">[137]</a></sup> Calligraphy would use a variety of stylised and standardised scripts, two major scripts among them being <i><a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">kufic</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Naskh_(script)" title="Naskh (script)">naskh</a>.</i> Ceramics, metalwork and glassware were also brilliantly decorated with geometric patterns and vibrant colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Manuscript illumination was an important art, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> painting flourished in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persianate_world" title="Persianate world">Persianate world</a>, and went on to influence miniature art in the Ottoman and Mughal court between the 16th–17th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> Very few surviving records of wall painting exists, especially ones that represented the human face. A rare example of this are the early 9th-century fragments from the ruins of the Dar al-Khilafah palace at <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarra</a> from the Abbasid period. These are fragments of larger wall paintings depicting harem women, period-era clothing and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a></div>
<p>The ninth and tenth centuries saw a flowering of Arabic music. Philosopher and esthete <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> at the end of the ninth century, established the foundations of modern Arabic music theory, based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_Maqam" title="Arab Maqam">maqammat</a>, or musical modes. His work was based on the music of <a href="/wiki/Ziryab" title="Ziryab">Ziryab</a>, the court musician of Andalusia. Ziryab was a renowned polymath, whose contributions to western civilization included formal dining, haircuts, chess, and more, in addition to his dominance of the world musical scene of the ninth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sumerians and Akkadians, the Greeks, and the Persians all used math to create notes used on lutes and lyres and other stringed instruments. Using the idea that a plucked or bowed string produces a note, they noticed the difference in tone when a string is stopped. "The great discovery" was hearing the double octave, that halving a string produces a note one octave above the string.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacClean&amp;Dumbrill_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacClean&amp;Dumbrill-146">[144]</a></sup> Written as a ratio 2:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacClean&amp;Dumbrill_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacClean&amp;Dumbrill-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>They measured the ratios of string lengths on one side and the other of where the string was pressed, creating ratios. Those ratios allowed them to compare sounds, for example third intervals, fourths, fifths. They were able to tune one string against another in those intervals on lutes, lyres, harps, zithers. Lutes gave them the further ability to create those intervals on a single string, by adding frets at mathematically spaced distances, based on the ratios. Unlike modern instruments, where frets may be permanently fixed into the neck, as on a guitar, the older instruments used gut strings tied around the neck for frets, and this made their instruments adjustable. Early musicians could tune their instruments to different <a href="/wiki/Mode_(music)" title="Mode (music)">modes</a>. Lute players could tune the strings to different <a href="/wiki/Interval_(music)" title="Interval (music)">intervals</a>, and could further adjust the frets for the modes.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Safi_al-Din_lute.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1318" data-file-width="866" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Safi_al-Din_lute.png/220px-Safi_al-Din_lute.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Safi_al-Din_lute.png/330px-Safi_al-Din_lute.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Safi_al-Din_lute.png/440px-Safi_al-Din_lute.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Safi_al-Din_lute.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing of a lute by <a href="/wiki/Safi_al-Din_al-Urmawi" title="Safi al-Din al-Urmawi">Safi al-Din</a> from a 1333 copy of his book, <i>Kitab al-Adwār</i>. The oldest copy dates to 1296.</div></div></div>
<p>The mixing cultures of Central Asia and Arabia produced several thinkers who wrote about music, including something about the lute in their works, including <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a> (c. 801 – c. 873), <a href="/wiki/Ziryab" title="Ziryab">Ziryab</a> (789–857), <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a> (c. 872 – c. 950), <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> (c. 980 – 1037), and <a href="/wiki/Safi_al-Din_al-Urmawi" title="Safi al-Din al-Urmawi">Safi al-Din al-Urmawi</a> (1216–1294). They wrote in Arabic, what had become the useful lingua-Franca of their time, and took part in Muslim society and culture. However they were brought up in Central Asia.
</p><p>The Arabs had a musical scale, described by al-Farabi, in use by some through the 13th century A.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna2_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna2-147">[145]</a></sup> That tanbar scale, which divided the string into "40 equal parts" may have been a leftover from Babylon and Assyria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna2_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna2-147">[145]</a></sup> However, the Arabs traded with and conquered the Persians, and they adopted Persian scales for their lutes, just as they adopted Persian short-necked lutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna2_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna2-147">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ziryab" title="Ziryab">Ziryab</a> moved from Baghdad to <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>, where he set up a school of music and was one of the first to add a fifth string or course to oud, "between 822 and 852).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna-148">[146]</a></sup> Al-Andalus, where he settled would become a center of musical instrument development for Europe.
</p><p>Al-Kindi was a polymath who wrote as many as 15 music-related treatises. He was among the first to apply Greek musical theory to Central Asian-Arabian short lutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna-148">[146]</a></sup> He added semi-tones between the nut and the first string.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna-148">[146]</a></sup> He also added a fifth string to his oud in the east, as Ziryab had done in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farmerluteavicenna_148-3"><a href="#cite_note-farmerluteavicenna-148">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Farabi "fully incorporated the works of <a href="/wiki/Aristoxenus" title="Aristoxenus">Aristoxenus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> into his theory of tetrachords", and wrote among books in many subjects, the <a href="/wiki/Kitab_al-Musiqa_al-Kabir" title="Kitab al-Musiqa al-Kabir">Kitab al-Musiqa al-Kabir</a>, the <i>Major Book of Music</i>, in which he detailed how to tune an oud, using mathematical ratios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farabi1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-farabi1-149">[147]</a></sup> He gave instruction for both 10 frets and 12, telling where to place the tied (and moveable) gut-string frets on the neck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farabi1_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-farabi1-149">[147]</a></sup> His way of tuning allowed a "12-fret ‘ud tuning — which results ... 'double-octave' scale", with 22 notes in each octave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farabi1_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-farabi1-149">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Uqba" title="Mosque of Uqba">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>), the ancestor of all the mosques in the western Islamic world excluding Turkey and the Balkans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kairouan_mosque_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-kairouan_mosque-150">[148]</a></sup> is one of the best preserved and most significant examples of early great mosques. Founded in 670, it dates in its present form largely from the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> The Great Mosque of Kairouan is constituted of a three-tiered square minaret, a large courtyard surrounded by colonnaded porticos, and a huge <a href="/wiki/Hypostyle" title="Hypostyle">hypostyle</a> prayer hall covered on its axis by two cupolas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kairouan_mosque_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-kairouan_mosque-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Samarra" title="Great Mosque of Samarra">Great Mosque of Samarra</a> in Iraq was completed in 847. It combined the hypostyle architecture of rows of columns supporting a flat base, above which a huge spiralling <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minaret</a> was constructed.
</p><p>The beginning of construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral%E2%80%93Mosque_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Cathedral–Mosque of Córdoba">Great Mosque at Cordoba</a> in 785 marked the beginning of Islamic architecture in Spain and Northern Africa. The mosque is noted for its striking interior arches. Moorish architecture reached its peak with the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a>, the magnificent palace/fortress of <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>, with its open and breezy interior spaces adorned in red, blue, and gold. The walls are decorated with stylized foliage motifs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> inscriptions, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabesque_(Islamic_art)" title="Arabesque (Islamic art)">arabesque</a> design work, with walls covered in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns" title="Islamic geometric patterns">geometrically patterned</a> glazed tiles.
</p><p>Many traces of <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_architecture" title="Fatimid architecture">Fatimid architecture</a> exist in Cairo today, the most defining examples include the Al Azhar University and the <a href="/wiki/Al-Hakim_Mosque" title="Al-Hakim Mosque">Al Hakim mosque</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">Decline</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_factors">Cultural factors</span></h3>
<p>Economic historian Joel Mokyr has argued that Islamic philosopher <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a> (1058–1111), the author of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incoherence_of_the_Philosophers" title="Incoherence of the Philosophers">Incoherence of the Philosophers</a>, "was a key figure in the decline in Islamic science" and that this led to a cultural shift shunning away from scientific thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> However, it is argued that al-Ghazali was instead an admirer and adherent of philosophy but was criticizing the use of philosophy in religious matters only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> Additionally, Saliba (2007) has pointed out that the golden age did not slow down after al-Ghazali, who lived in the 11th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> while others extend the golden age to around the 16th<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-3">[3]</a></sup> to 17th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_and_economic_factors">Political and economic factors</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">Great Divergence</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Y._al-Hassan" title="Ahmad Y. al-Hassan">Ahmad Y. al-Hassan</a> has rejected the thesis that lack of creative thinking was a cause, arguing that science was always kept separate from religious argument; he instead analyzes the decline in terms of economic and political factors, drawing on the work of the 14th-century writer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several other contemporary scholars have analysed the decline in terms of political and economic factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saliba_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-King-2">[2]</a></sup> Current research has led to the conclusion that "the available evidence is consistent with the hypothesis that an increase in the political power of these elites caused the observed decline in scientific output."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> The decline could be part of a larger trend where the non-Western world fell behind the West in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">Great Divergence</a>. In 1206, <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> which, during the 13th century, conquered most of the Eurasian land mass, including China in the east and much of the old Islamic caliphate (as well as <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>) in the west. The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">destruction of Baghdad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a> in 1258 has been seen by some as the end of the Islamic Golden Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup> However, while cultural influence used to radiate outward from Baghdad, after the fall of Baghdad, Iran and Central Asia saw a cultural flourishing by benefiting from increased cross-cultural access to East Asia under <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Mongol rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulliet_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-bulliet-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subtelny_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-subtelny-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Economics-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Economics_111-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<ul><li>"He is considered by some as a father of modern economics, or at least a major forerunner. The Western world recognizes Khaldun as the father of sociology but hesitates in recognizing him as a great economist who laid its very foundations. He was the first to systematically analyze the functioning of an economy, the importance of technology, specialization and foreign trade in economic surplus and the role of government and its stabilization policies to increase output and employment. Moreover, he dealt with the problem of optimum taxation, minimum government services, incentives, institutional framework, law and order, expectations, production, and the theory of value".<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120311091356/http://www.ovidius-stec.ro/html/anale/ENG/cuprins%20rezumate/2009%20vol2.pdf" rel="nofollow">Cosma, Sorinel (2009). "Ibn Khaldun's Economic Thinking". Ovidius University Annals of Economics (Ovidius University Press) XIV:52–57</a></li></ul>
</span></li>
